<p><b>Huawei Technologies Co., Ltd.</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/hw/: &#39;wh&#39; in &#39;why&#39;">hw</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">HWAH</span>-way</i></a>; <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh">华为</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn"><span class="ext-phonos skin-invert"><span data-nosnippet="" id="ooui-php-2" class="ext-phonos-PhonosButton noexcerpt oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-buttonElement oo-ui-buttonElement-frameless oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement oo-ui-buttonWidget" data-ooui="{&quot;_&quot;:&quot;mw.Phonos.PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;href&quot;:&quot;\/\/upload.wikimedia.org\/wikipedia\/commons\/transcoded\/a\/a0\/Huawei_pronunciation.ogg\/Huawei_pronunciation.ogg.mp3&quot;,&quot;rel&quot;:[&quot;nofollow&quot;],&quot;framed&quot;:false,&quot;icon&quot;:&quot;volumeUp&quot;,&quot;label&quot;:{&quot;html&quot;:&quot;Hu\u00e1w\u00e9i&quot;},&quot;data&quot;:{&quot;ipa&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;lang&quot;:&quot;en&quot;,&quot;wikibase&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;file&quot;:&quot;Huawei pronunciation.ogg&quot;},&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;noexcerpt&quot;]}"><a role="button" tabindex="0" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a0/Huawei_pronunciation.ogg/Huawei_pronunciation.ogg.mp3" rel="nofollow" aria-label="Play audio" title="Play audio" class="oo-ui-buttonElement-button"><span class="oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-volumeUp"></span><span class="oo-ui-labelElement-label">Huáwéi</span><span class="oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicatorElement-noIndicator"></span></a></span><sup class="ext-phonos-attribution noexcerpt navigation-not-searchable"><a href="/wiki/File:Huawei_pronunciation.ogg" title="File:Huawei pronunciation.ogg">ⓘ</a></sup></span></span></i>) is a Chinese <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational</a> <a href="/wiki/Digital_communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Digital communications">digital communications</a> technology <a href="/wiki/Conglomerate_(company)" title="Conglomerate (company)">conglomerate</a> corporation headquartered in Bantian, Longgang District, Shenzhen, <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>. It designs, develops, manufactures and sells <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_equipment" title="Telecommunications equipment">telecommunications equipment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consumer_electronics" title="Consumer electronics">consumer electronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_device" title="Smart device">smart devices</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_solar_power" title="Rooftop solar power">rooftop solar</a> products. The corporation was founded in 1987 by <a href="/wiki/Ren_Zhengfei" title="Ren Zhengfei">Ren Zhengfei</a>, a former officer in the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People&#39;s Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a> (PLA).<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially focused on manufacturing <a href="/wiki/Stored_program_control" title="Stored program control">phone switches</a>, Huawei has expanded to more than 170 countries to include building telecommunications networks, providing operational and consulting services and equipment, and manufacturing communications devices for the consumer market.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It overtook <a href="/wiki/Ericsson" title="Ericsson">Ericsson</a> in 2012 as the largest telecommunications equipment manufacturer in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Huawei surpassed <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Electronics" title="Samsung Electronics">Samsung</a>, in 2018 and 2020, respectively, to become the largest smartphone manufacturer worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Business_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Business-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Amidst its rise, Huawei has been accused of intellectual property infringement, for which it has settled with companies like <a href="/wiki/Cisco" title="Cisco">Cisco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:19_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:19-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Questions regarding the extent of state influence on Huawei have revolved around its <a href="/wiki/National_champions" title="National champions">national champions</a> role in China, subsidies and financing support from state entities,<sup id="cite_ref-:2_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and reactions of the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_China" title="Government of China">Chinese government</a> in light of oppositions in certain countries to Huawei's participation in <a href="/wiki/5G" title="5G">5G</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto16_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Its software and equipment have been linked to the <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_China" title="Mass surveillance in China">mass surveillance</a> of <a href="/wiki/Uyghurs" title="Uyghurs">Uyghurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_internment_camps" title="Xinjiang internment camps">Xinjiang internment camps</a>, drawing sanctions from the US.<sup id="cite_ref-auto13_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto13-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto15_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto15-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The company has faced difficulties in some countries arising from <a href="/wiki/Concerns_over_Chinese_involvement_in_5G_wireless_networks" title="Concerns over Chinese involvement in 5G wireless networks">concerns</a> that its equipment may enable surveillance by the Chinese government due to perceived connections with the country's military and intelligence agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Huawei has argued that critics such as the US government have not shown evidence of espionage.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Experts say that China's 2014 Counter-Espionage Law and 2017 <a href="/wiki/National_Intelligence_Law_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="National Intelligence Law of the People&#39;s Republic of China">National Intelligence Law</a> can compel Huawei and other companies to cooperate with state intelligence.<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, Australian and US intelligence agencies concluded that a hack on Australia's telecom networks was conducted by or through Huawei, although the two network operators have disputed that information.<sup id="cite_ref-bnnbloomberg.ca_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bnnbloomberg.ca-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-news.com.au_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.com.au-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the midst of a <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93United_States_trade_war" title="China–United States trade war">trade war between China and the United States</a>, the US government alleged that Huawei had violated <a href="/wiki/United_States_sanctions_against_Iran" title="United States sanctions against Iran">sanctions against Iran</a> and restricted it from doing business with American companies. In June 2019, Huawei cut jobs at its <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara,_California" title="Santa Clara, California">Santa Clara</a> research center, and in December Ren Zhengfei said it was moving to <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In 2020, Huawei agreed to sell the <a href="/wiki/Honor_(brand)" title="Honor (brand)">Honor</a> brand to a <a href="/wiki/State-owned_enterprise" title="State-owned enterprise">state-owned enterprise</a> of the Shenzhen government to "ensure its survival" under US sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawler_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawler-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In November 2022, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC) banned sales or import of equipment made by Huawei out of national security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Other countries such as <a href="/wiki/Quadrilateral_Security_Dialogue" title="Quadrilateral Security Dialogue">Quad</a> members India and Japan, members of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Eyes" title="Five Eyes">Five Eyes</a>, and ten <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> states have also banned or restricted Huawei products.<sup id="cite_ref-Japan_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Japan-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-India_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-India-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Europeancountries_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europeancountries-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the company founder Ren Zhengfei, the name <i>Huawei</i> comes from a slogan he saw on a wall, <i>Zhonghua youwei</i> meaning "China has promise" (<span lang="zh">中华有为</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Zhōng huá yǒu wéi</span></i>), when he was starting up the company and needed a name.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <i>Zhonghua</i> or <i>Hua</i> means China,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> while <i>youwei</i> means "promising/to show promise".<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <i>Huawei</i> has also been translated as "splendid achievement" or "China is able", which are possible readings of the name.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Chinese <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>, the name is <i>Huáwéi</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="cmn-Latn-fonipa" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">[xwa&#780;we&#780;ɪ]</a></span> in <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, the name is transliterated with <a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a> as <i>Waa<sup>4</sup>-wai<sup>4</sup></i> and pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="yue-Latn-fonipa" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">[waː˩wɐi˩]</a></span>. However, the pronunciation of <i>Huawei</i> by non-Chinese varies in other countries, for example "Hoe-ah-wei" in Belgium and the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-segers_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-segers-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The company had considered changing the name in English out of concern that non-Chinese people may find it hard to pronounce,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> but decided to keep the name, and launched a name recognition campaign instead to encourage a pronunciation closer to "Wah-Way" using the words "Wow Way".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Ren states, "We will not change the name of our brand and will teach foreigners how to pronounce it. We have to make sure they do not pronounce it like 'Hawaii.'"<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 85">&#58;&#8202;85&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early years"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1980s, the Chinese government endeavored to overhaul the nation's underdeveloped telecommunications infrastructure. A core component of the telecommunications network was telephone exchange switches, and in the late 1980s, several Chinese research groups endeavored to acquire and develop the technology, usually through joint ventures with foreign companies.
</p><p>Ren Zhengfei, a former deputy director of the People's Liberation Army engineering corps, founded Huawei in 1987 in Shenzhen. The company reports that it had <a href="/wiki/RMB" class="mw-redirect" title="RMB">RMB</a> 21,000 (about $5,000 at the time) in registered capital from Ren Zhengfei and five other investors at the time of its founding where each contributed RMB 3,500.<sup id="cite_ref-Ash_Center_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ash_Center-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> These five initial investors gradually withdrew their investments in Huawei. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> has suggested, however, that Huawei received approximately "$46 billion in loans and other support, coupled with $25 billion in tax cuts" since the Chinese government had a vested interest in fostering a company to compete against Apple and Samsung.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Huawei_denies_receiving_billions_in_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huawei_denies_receiving_billions_in-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ren sought to <a href="/wiki/Reverse-engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Reverse-engineering">reverse engineer</a> foreign technologies with local researchers. China borrowed liberally from Qualcomm and other industry leaders (PBX as an example) in order to enter the market. At a time when all of China's telecommunications technology was imported from abroad, Ren hoped to build a domestic Chinese telecommunications company that could compete with, and ultimately replace, foreign competitors.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During its first several years the company's business model consisted mainly of reselling <a href="/wiki/Private_branch_exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Private branch exchange">private branch exchange</a> (PBX) switches imported from Hong Kong.<sup id="cite_ref-CSIS_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSIS-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, it was reverse-engineering imported switches and investing heavily in research and development to manufacture its own technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-CSIS_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSIS-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> By 1990 the company had approximately 600 R&amp;D staff and began its own independent commercialization of PBX switches targeting hotels and small enterprises.<sup id="cite_ref-Huawei-milestones_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huawei-milestones-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In order to grow despite difficult competition from <a href="/wiki/Alcatel-Lucent" title="Alcatel-Lucent">Alcatel</a>, Lucent, and <a href="/wiki/Nortel_Networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Nortel Networks">Nortel Networks</a>, in 1992 Huawei focused on low-income and difficult to access market niches.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 12">&#58;&#8202;12&#8202;</span></sup> Huawei's sales force traveled from village to village in underdeveloped regions, gradually moving into more developed areas.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 12">&#58;&#8202;12&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>The company's first major breakthrough came in 1993 when it launched its C&amp;C08 program controlled telephone switch. It was by far the most powerful switch available in China at the time. By initially deploying in small cities and rural areas and placing emphasis on service and customizability, the company gained market share and made its way into the mainstream market.<sup id="cite_ref-Business-Today_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Business-Today-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei also won a key contract to build the first national telecommunications network for the People's Liberation Army, a deal one employee described as "small in terms of our overall business, but large in terms of our relationships".<sup id="cite_ref-FEAR_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEAR-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In 1994, founder Ren Zhengfei had a meeting with <a href="/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Chinese_Communist_Party" title="General Secretary of the Chinese Communist Party">General Secretary of the Chinese Communist Party</a> <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Zemin" title="Jiang Zemin">Jiang Zemin</a>, telling him that "switching equipment technology was related to national security, and that a nation that did not have its own switching equipment was like one that lacked its own military." Jiang reportedly agreed with this assessment.<sup id="cite_ref-CSIS_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSIS-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1990s, Canadian telecom giant <a href="/wiki/Nortel" title="Nortel">Nortel</a> outsourced production of their entire product line to Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-Kehoe_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kehoe-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> They subsequently outsourced much of their product engineering to Huawei as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another major turning point for the company came in 1996 when the government in Beijing adopted an explicit policy of supporting domestic telecommunications manufacturers and restricting access to foreign competitors. Huawei was promoted by both the government and the military as a national champion, and established new research and development offices.<sup id="cite_ref-CSIS_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSIS-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_expansion">Foreign expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Foreign expansion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">In <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">Vilnius</a>, Lithuania</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" href="/wiki/Voorburg" title="Voorburg">Voorburg</a>, Netherlands</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:HuaweiCanada7.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/HuaweiCanada7.jpg/200px-HuaweiCanada7.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="132" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/HuaweiCanada7.jpg/300px-HuaweiCanada7.jpg 1.5x, href="/wiki/Markham,_Ontario" title="Markham, Ontario">Markham</a>, Ontario, Canada</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in the late 1990s, Huawei built communications networks throughout <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> It has become the most important Chinese telecommunications company operating in these regions.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1997, Huawei won a contract to provide fixed-line network products to Hong Kong company <a href="/wiki/Hutchison_Whampoa" title="Hutchison Whampoa">Hutchison Whampoa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Business-Today_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Business-Today-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Later that year, Huawei launched wireless <a href="/wiki/GSM" title="GSM">GSM</a>-based products and eventually expanded to offer <a href="/wiki/CDMA" class="mw-redirect" title="CDMA">CDMA</a> and <a href="/wiki/UMTS" title="UMTS">UMTS</a>. In 1999, the company opened a research and development (R&amp;D) centre in <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bengaluru</a>, India to develop a wide range of telecom software.<sup id="cite_ref-Huawei-milestones_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huawei-milestones-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2003, Huawei partnered with <a href="/wiki/3Com" title="3Com">3Com</a> on a joint venture known as H3C, which was focused on enterprise networking equipment. It marked 3Com's re-entrance into the high-end core routers and switch market, after having abandoned it in 2000 to focus on other businesses. 3Com bought out Huawei's share of the venture in 2006 for US$882 million.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, Huawei signed a $10 billion credit line with <a href="/wiki/China_Development_Bank" title="China Development Bank">China Development Bank</a> to provide low-cost financing to customers buying its telecommunications equipment to support its sales outside of China. This line of credit was tripled to $30 billion in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Mcmorrow_2019_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mcmorrow_2019-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, Huawei's foreign contract orders exceeded its domestic sales for the first time. Huawei signed a global <a href="/wiki/Framework_agreement" title="Framework agreement">framework agreement</a> with <a href="/wiki/Vodafone" title="Vodafone">Vodafone</a>. This agreement marked the first time a telecommunications equipment supplier from China had received Approved Supplier status from Vodafone Global Supply Chain.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (May 2019)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In 2007, Huawei began a joint venture with US security software vendor <a href="/wiki/Symantec_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Symantec Corporation">Symantec Corporation</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Huawei_Symantec" title="Huawei Symantec">Huawei Symantec</a>, which aimed to provide end-to-end solutions for network data storage and security. Huawei bought out Symantec's share in the venture in 2012, with <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> noting that Symantec had fears that the partnership "would prevent it from obtaining United States government classified information about cyber threats".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2008, Australian carrier <a href="/wiki/Optus" title="Optus">Optus</a> announced that it would establish a technology research facility with Huawei in <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In October 2008, Huawei reached an agreement to contribute to a new GSM-based <a href="/wiki/HSPA%2B" class="mw-redirect" title="HSPA+">HSPA+</a> network being deployed jointly by Canadian carriers <a href="/wiki/Bell_Mobility" title="Bell Mobility">Bell Mobility</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telus_Mobility" title="Telus Mobility">Telus Mobility</a>, joined by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nokia_Siemens_Networks%27%27&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nokia Siemens Networks&#39;&#39; (page does not exist)">Nokia Siemens Networks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Huawei delivered one of the world's first <a href="/wiki/3GPP_Long_Term_Evolution" class="mw-redirect" title="3GPP Long Term Evolution">LTE</a>/EPC commercial networks for <a href="/wiki/TeliaSonera" class="mw-redirect" title="TeliaSonera">TeliaSonera</a> in Oslo, Norway in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Huawei-milestones_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huawei-milestones-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Norway-based telecommunications <a href="/wiki/Telenor" title="Telenor">Telenor</a> instead selected <a href="/wiki/Ericsson" title="Ericsson">Ericsson</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Huawei#Espionage_and_security_concerns" title="Criticism of Huawei">security concerns</a> with Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2010, Huawei was included in the <a href="/wiki/Fortune_Global_500" title="Fortune Global 500">Global Fortune 500</a> 2010 list published by the US magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> for the first time, on the strength of annual sales of US$21.8 billion and net profit of US$2.67 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2012, it was announced that Huawei would move its UK headquarters to <a href="/wiki/Green_Park_Business_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Park Business Park">Green Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading, Berkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei also has expanding operations in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> since 2016. As well as a headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, it has facilities in Cork and Westmeath.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2017, Huawei created a <a href="/wiki/Narrowband_IoT" title="Narrowband IoT">Narrowband IoT</a> city-aware network using a "one network, one platform, N applications" construction model utilizing <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_things" title="Internet of things">Internet of things</a> (IoT), <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">cloud computing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Big_data" title="Big data">big data</a>, and other next-generation <a href="/wiki/Information_and_communications_technology" title="Information and communications technology">information and communications technology</a>, it also aims to be one of the world's five largest cloud players in the near future.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, Huawei and the government of Malaysia began cooperating to develop public security programs and Malaysian <a href="/wiki/Smart_city" title="Smart city">Smart City</a> programs, as well as a related lab in <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 82">&#58;&#8202;82&#8202;</span></sup> In April 2019, Huawei established the Huawei Malaysia Global Training Centre (MGTC) at <a href="/wiki/Cyberjaya" title="Cyberjaya">Cyberjaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei has had a major role in building, by 2019, approximately 70% of Africa's 4G networks.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 76">&#58;&#8202;76&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>In November 2020, <a href="/wiki/Telus_Mobility" title="Telus Mobility">Telus Mobility</a> dropped Huawei in favor of Samsung, Ericsson, and Nokia for their 5G/<a href="/wiki/Radio_access_network" title="Radio access network">Radio Access Network</a><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_performance">Recent performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Recent performance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px
