<p>By 2018, Huawei had sold 200 million smartphones.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 2019, Huawei reported revenue of US$122 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> By the second quarter of 2020, Huawei had become the world's top smartphone seller, overtaking <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Electronics" title="Samsung Electronics">Samsung</a> for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-Business_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Business-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 2021, Huawei was ranked the second-largest R&amp;D investor in the world by the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Research_Centre" title="Joint Research Centre">EU Joint Research Centre (JRC)</a> in its EU Industrial R&amp;D Investment Scoreboard<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> and ranked fifth in the world in US patents according to a report by Fairview Research's IFI Claims Patent Services.<sup id="cite_ref-FairviewResearchHuaweiNo5USPatents_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FairviewResearchHuaweiNo5USPatents-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 10">&#58;&#8202;10&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>However, heavy international sanctions saw Huawei's revenues drop by 32% in the 2021 third quarter.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Linghao Bao, an analyst at policy research firm Trivium China said the "communications giant went from being the second-largest smartphone maker in the world, after Samsung, to essentially dead."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> By the end of third quarter in 2022, Huawei revenue had dropped a further 19.7% since the beginning of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_affairs">Corporate affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Corporate affairs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Huawei classifies itself as a "<a href="/wiki/Collective" title="Collective">collective</a>" entity and prior to 2019 did not refer to itself as a private company. <a href="/wiki/Richard_McGregor" title="Richard McGregor">Richard McGregor</a>, author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Party:_The_Secret_World_of_China%27s_Communist_Rulers" title="The Party: The Secret World of China&#39;s Communist Rulers">The Party: The Secret World of China's Communist Rulers</a></i>, said that this is "a definitional distinction that has been essential to the company's receipt of state support at crucial points in its development".<sup id="cite_ref-McGregor204_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGregor204-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> McGregor argued that "Huawei's status as a genuine collective is doubtful."<sup id="cite_ref-McGregor204_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGregor204-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Huawei's position has shifted in 2019 when, Dr. Song Liuping, Huawei's chief legal officer, commented on the US government ban, said: "Politicians in the US are using the strength of an entire nation to come after <i>a private company</i>." (emphasis added).<sup id="cite_ref-CNET_2019_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNET_2019-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Leadership"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ren Zhengfei is the founder and CEO of Huawei and has the power to veto any decisions made by the board of directors.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarke_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Huawei also has rotating co-CEOs.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 11">&#58;&#8202;11&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Huawei disclosed its list of board of directors for the first time in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Directors-2010_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Directors-2010-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liang_Hua" title="Liang Hua">Liang Hua</a> is the current chair of the board. As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the members of the board are Liang Hua, Guo Ping, Xu Zhijun, Hu Houkun, <a href="/wiki/Meng_Wanzhou" title="Meng Wanzhou">Meng Wanzhou</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chief_financial_officer" title="Chief financial officer">CFO</a> and deputy chairwoman), Ding Yun, Yu Chengdong, Wang Tao, Xu Wenwei, Shen-Han Chiu, Chen Lifang, Peng Zhongyang, He Tingbo, Li Yingtao, Ren Zhengfei, Yao Fuhai, Tao Jingwen, and Yan Lida.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Guo Ping is the Chairman of Huawei Device, Huawei's mobile phone division.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Huawei's <a href="/wiki/Chief_compliance_officer" title="Chief compliance officer">Chief Ethics &amp; Compliance Officer</a> is Zhou Daiqi<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> who is also Huawei's <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party_Committee_Secretary" title="Chinese Communist Party Committee Secretary">Chinese Communist Party Committee Secretary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TT_Mo_Han_Aw_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TT_Mo_Han_Aw-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Their <a href="/wiki/Chief_legal_officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief legal officer">chief legal officer</a> is Song Liuping.<sup id="cite_ref-CNET_2019_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNET_2019-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership">Ownership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ownership"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At its founding in 1987, Huawei was established as a collectively-owned enterprise.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 213">&#58;&#8202;213&#8202;</span></sup> Collectively-owned enterprises were an intermediary corporate ownership status between <a href="/wiki/State-owned_enterprises_of_China" title="State-owned enterprises of China">state-owned enterprises</a> and private businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 213">&#58;&#8202;213&#8202;</span></sup> The Chinese government began issuing licenses for private businesses starting in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 213">&#58;&#8202;213&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Huawei states it is an <a href="/wiki/Employee_stock_ownership" title="Employee stock ownership">employee-owned company</a>, but this remains a point of dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarke_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Ren Zhengfei retains approximately 1 percent of the shares of Huawei's holding company, Huawei Investment &amp; Holding,<sup id="cite_ref-:5_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> with the remainder of the shares held by a trade union <i>committee</i> (not a <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade union</a> per se, and the internal governance procedures of this committee, its members, its leaders or how they are selected all remain undisclosed to the public) that is claimed to be representative of Huawei's employee shareholders.<sup id="cite_ref-Clarke_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yang_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yang-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The company's trade union committee is registered with and pays dues to the Shenzhen federation of the <a href="/wiki/All-China_Federation_of_Trade_Unions" title="All-China Federation of Trade Unions">All-China Federation of Trade Unions</a>, which is controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Chinese Communist Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> About half of Huawei staff participate in this structure (foreign employees are not eligible), and hold what the company calls "virtual restricted shares". These shares are non-tradable and are allocated to reward performance.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> When employees leave Huawei, their shares revert to the company, which compensates them for their holding.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Although employee shareholders receive dividends,<sup id="cite_ref-Yang_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yang-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> their shares do not entitle them to any direct influence in management decisions, but enables them to vote for members of the 115-person Representatives' Commission from a pre-selected list of candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-Yang_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yang-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The Representatives' Commission selects Huawei Holding's board of directors and Board of Supervisors.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Academics Christopher Balding of <a href="/wiki/Fulbright_University_Vietnam" title="Fulbright University Vietnam">Fulbright University</a> and Donald C. Clarke of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a> have described Huawei's virtual stock program as "purely a profit-sharing incentive scheme" that "has nothing to do with financing or control".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> They found that, after a few stages of historical morphing, employees do not own a part of Huawei through their shares. Instead, the "virtual stock is a contract right, not a property right; it gives the holder <a href="/wiki/Non-voting_stock" title="Non-voting stock">no voting power</a> in either Huawei Tech or Huawei Holding, cannot be transferred, and is cancelled when the employee leaves the firm, subject to a redemption payment from Huawei Holding TUC at a low fixed price".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clarke_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Balding and Clarke add, "given the public nature of trade unions in China, if the ownership stake of the trade union committee is genuine, and if the trade union and its committee function as trade unions generally function in China, then Huawei may be deemed effectively state-owned."<sup id="cite_ref-Clarke_76-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Tim Rühlig, a Research Fellow at the Swedish Institute of International Affairs, asked Huawei for a response to the Balding and Clarke paper; the "information provided by Huawei gives an indication of how difficult it is to run an independent company in such a crucial sector in China".<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> After the publication of Balding and Clarke's paper, Huawei has "engaged in a PR blitz to manufacture an image of a transparent company".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Academic Toshio Goto of the <a href="/wiki/Japan_University_of_Economics" title="Japan University of Economics">Japan University of Economics</a> has disagreed with Balding and Clarke's assessment of Huawei employee shareholders’ ownership.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 13">&#58;&#8202;13&#8202;</span></sup> Goto writes that the Huawei's ownership structure is a function of its formation amid the Chinese reforms, with the only mechanism for concentrating employee ownership under Shenzen's 1997 Provisions on State-owned Company Employee Stock Option Plans being to do so via Huawei's trade union.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 25">&#58;&#8202;25&#8202;</span></sup> In contrast to Balding and Clarke, Goto writes that the Huawei's virtual shares are substantially equivalent to voting stock, and that nominal ownership through the trade union does not change the legal and financial independence of employee ownership from the union itself.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 25">&#58;&#8202;25&#8202;</span></sup> Goto concludes that the firm is effectively owned by employees and therefore it is not effectively state-owned.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 25">&#58;&#8202;25&#8202;</span></sup> In analyzing Huawei's corporate governance and ownership structure, Academic Wang Jun of the Chinese University of Politics and Law also rejects the argument that Huawei is a state-owned enterprise controlled by a labor union, writing that normative practices and legal requirements distinguish between the shareholding vehicle of union-held employee assets and assets belonging to the union itself.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Academics Kunyuan Qiao of <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marquis" title="Christopher Marquis">Christopher Marquis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> likewise conclude that Huawei is a private company owned collectively by its employees and is neither owned nor controlled directly by the Chinese government.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 11">&#58;&#8202;11&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Academics <a href="/wiki/Steve_Tsang" title="Steve Tsang">Steve Tsang</a> and Olivia Cheung write that Huawei is a private company.<sup id="cite_ref-:Tsang&amp;Cheung_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Tsang&amp;Cheung-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 131">&#58;&#8202;131&#8202;</span></sup> Likewise, academics Simon Curtis and Ian Klaus write that Huawei is not state-owned, but is a private company which the Chinese government views as a national champion.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus3_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus3-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 156–157">&#58;&#8202;156–157&#8202;</span></sup> 
</p><p>In 2021, Huawei did not report its ultimate <a href="/wiki/Beneficial_ownership" title="Beneficial ownership">beneficial ownership</a> in Europe as required by European anti-<a href="/wiki/Money_laundering" title="Money laundering">money laundering</a> laws.<sup id="cite_ref-Šimalčík_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Šimalčík-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lobbying_and_public_relations">Lobbying and public relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Lobbying and public relations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In July 2021, Huawei hired <a href="/wiki/Tony_Podesta" title="Tony Podesta">Tony Podesta</a> as a consultant and lobbyist, with a goal of nurturing the company's relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Joe_Biden" title="Presidency of Joe Biden">Biden administration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei has also hired <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">public relations</a> firms <a href="/wiki/Ruder_Finn" title="Ruder Finn">Ruder Finn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wavemaker_(media_agency)" title="Wavemaker (media agency)">Wavemaker</a>, Racepoint Global, and <a href="/wiki/Burson_Cohn_%26_Wolfe" title="Burson Cohn &amp; Wolfe">Burson Cohn &amp; Wolfe</a> for various campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2024, Bloomberg News reported that Huawei ended its in-house lobbying operations in Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_culture">Corporate culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Corporate culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to its CEO and founder Ren Zhengfei, Huawei's corporate culture is the same as the culture of the CCP, "and to <a href="/wiki/Serve_the_People" title="Serve the People">serve the people</a> wholeheartedly means to be customer-centric and responsible to society."<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 9">&#58;&#8202;9&#8202;</span></sup> Ren frequently states that Huawei's management philosophy and strategy are commercial applications of <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">Maoism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 11">&#58;&#8202;11&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Ren states that in the event of a conflict between Huawei's business interests and the CCP's interests, he would "choose the CCP whose interest is to serve the people and all human beings".<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 11">&#58;&#8202;11&#8202;</span></sup> Qiao and Marquis observe that company founder Ren is a dedicated communist who seeks to ingrain communist values at Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 9">&#58;&#8202;9&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Partners">Partners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Partners"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Huawei_P9_main_camera.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Huawei_P9_main_camera.jpg/220px-Huawei_P9_main_camera.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Huawei_P9_main_camera.jpg/330px-Huawei_P9_main_camera.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Huawei_P9_main_camera.jpg/440px-Huawei_P9_main_camera.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3500" data-file-height="2336" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Huawei_P9" title="Huawei P9">Huawei P9</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a> to be co-engineered with a <a href="/wiki/Leica_Camera" title="Leica Camera">Leica camera</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>As of the beginning of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, approximately 80% of the world's top 50 telecoms companies had worked with Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-RCR_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RCR-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, German camera company <a href="/wiki/Leica_Camera" title="Leica Camera">Leica</a> has established a partnership with Huawei, and Leica cameras will be co-engineered into Huawei smartphones, including the P and Mate Series. The first smartphone to be co-engineered with a Leica camera was the <a href="/wiki/Huawei_P9" title="Huawei P9">Huawei P9</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> As of May 2022, Huawei partnership with Leica had ended.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> As of 2023, smartphone makers <a href="/wiki/Honor_(brand)" title="Honor (brand)">Honor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xiaomi" title="Xiaomi">Xiaomi</a> have partnerships with Leica.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2019, Huawei collaborated with eyewear company <a href="/wiki/Gentle_Monster" title="Gentle Monster">Gentle Monster</a> and released <a href="/wiki/Smartglasses" title="Smartglasses">smartglasses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In November 2019, Huawei partners with <a href="/wiki/Devialet" title="Devialet">Devialet</a> and unveiled a new specifically designed speaker, the Sound X.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In October 2020, Huawei released its own mapping service, <a href="/wiki/Petal_Maps" title="Petal Maps">Petal Maps</a>, which was developed in partnership with Dutch navigation device manufacturer <a href="/wiki/TomTom" title="TomTom">TomTom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Products_and_services">Products and services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Products and services"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Telecommunication_networks">Telecommunication networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Telecommunication networks"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huawei offers mobile and fixed <a href="/wiki/Softswitch" title="Softswitch">softswitches</a>, plus next-generation <a href="/wiki/Network_switching_subsystem#Home_location_register" title="Network switching subsystem">home location register</a> and <a href="/wiki/IP_Multimedia_Subsystem" title="IP Multimedia Subsystem">Internet Protocol Multimedia Subsystems</a> (IMS). Huawei sells <a href="/wiki/Digital_subscriber_line" title="Digital subscriber line">xDSL</a>, <a href="/wiki/Passive_optical_network" title="Passive optical network">passive optical network</a> (PON) and next-generation PON (NG PON) on a single platform. The company also offers mobile infrastructure, broadband access and service provider routers and switches (SPRS). Huawei's software products include <a href="/wiki/Service_delivery_platform" title="Service delivery platform">service delivery platforms</a> (SDPs), <a href="/wiki/Base_station_subsystem" title="Base station subsystem">base station subsystems</a>, and more.<sup id="cite_ref-Company_Information_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Company_Information-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiber-optic_cable_projects">Fiber-optic cable projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fiber-optic cable projects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huawei Marine Networks delivered the <a href="/wiki/List_of_international_submarine_communications_cables" title="List of international submarine communications cables">HANNIBAL</a> <a href="/wiki/Submarine_communications_cable" title="Submarine communications cable">submarine communications cable</a> system for <a href="/wiki/Tunisie_Telecom" title="Tunisie Telecom">Tunisie Telecom</a> across the Mediterranean Sea to Italy in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-:322_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:322-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 310">&#58;&#8202;310&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Huawei Marine is involved in many  fiber-optic cable projects connected with the <a href="/wiki/Belt_and_Road_Initiative" title="Belt and Road Initiative">Belt and Road Initiative</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 78">&#58;&#8202;78&#8202;</span></sup> Huawei Marine completed the China-Pakistan Fiber Optic Project which runs along the <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Pakistan_Economic_Corridor" title="China–Pakistan Economic Corridor">China-Pakistan Economic Corridor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 78">&#58;&#8202;78&#8202;</span></sup> In 2018, Huawei Marine completed the South Atlantic Interlink (SAIL) Cable System which runs from <a href="/wiki/Kribi" title="Kribi">Kribi</a>, Cameroon to <a href="/wiki/Fortaleza" title="Fortaleza">Fortaleza</a>, Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 78">&#58;&#8202;78&#8202;</span></sup> It also built the Kumul Domestic Fiber Cable from Indonesia to Papua New Guinea.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 78">&#58;&#8202;78&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>As part of the Smart Africa project, Huawei Marine built the 2,800 mile fiber-optic network Guinea Backbone Network.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus_64-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 78">&#58;&#8202;78&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_services">Global services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Global services"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huawei Global Services provides telecommunications operators with equipment to build and operate networks as well as consulting and engineering services to improve operational efficiencies.<sup id="cite_ref-MobileMag_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MobileMag-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/System_integration" title="System integration">network integration services</a> such as those for mobile and fixed networks; <a href="/wiki/Assurance_services" title="Assurance services">assurance services</a> such as network safety; and learning services, such as competency consulting.<sup id="cite_ref-Company_Information_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Company_Information-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Devices">Devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Devices"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Huawei_Band_7_in_Wilderness_Green_color.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Huawei_Band_7_in_Wilderness_Green_color.jpg/220px-Huawei_Band_7_in_Wilderness_Green_color.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="266" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Huawei_Band_7_in_Wilderness_Green_color.jpg/330px-Huawei_Band_7_in_Wilderness_Green_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Huawei_Band_7_in_Wilderness_Green_color.jpg/440px-Huawei_Band_7_in_Wilderness_Green_color.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1669" data-file-height="2016" /></a><figcaption>A Huawei Band 7 fitness tracker in Wilderness Green colour</figcaption></figure>
<p>Huawei's Devices division provides <a href="/wiki/White-label_product" title="White-label product">white-label products</a> to content-service providers, including <a href="/wiki/USB_modem" class="mw-redirect" title="USB modem">USB modems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modem#Radio" title="Modem">wireless modems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wireless_router" title="Wireless router">wireless routers</a> for mobile Wi-Fi,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Embedded_System_Module" title="Embedded System Module">embedded modules</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fixed_wireless" title="Fixed wireless">fixed wireless</a> terminals, <a href="/wiki/Wireless_gateway" title="Wireless gateway">wireless gateways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Set-top_box" title="Set-top box">set-top boxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile handsets</a> and video products.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Huawei also produces and sells a variety of devices under its own name, such as the smartphones, <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet PCs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huawei_FreeBuds" title="Huawei FreeBuds">earbuds</a> and Huawei Smartwatch.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phones">Phones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Phones"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Huawei_phones" title="List of Huawei phones">List of Huawei phones</a></div>
<p>Huawei is the second-biggest smartphone maker in the world, after <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a>, as of the first quarter of 2019. Their portfolio of phones includes both high-end smartphones, its <a href="/wiki/Huawei_Mate_series" title="Huawei Mate series">Huawei Mate series</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huawei_Pura_series" title="Huawei Pura series">Huawei Pura series</a>, and cheaper handsets that fall under its Honor brand.<sup id="cite_ref-Villas-Boas_Eadicicco_2019_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Villas-Boas_Eadicicco_2019-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cheaper handsets fall under its Honor brand.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Honor was created in order to elevate Huawei-branded phones as premium offerings. In 2020, Huawei agreed to sell the Honor brand to a <a href="/wiki/State-owned_enterprise" title="State-owned enterprise">state-owned enterprise</a> of the Shenzhen municipal government. Consequently, Honor was initially reported to be cut off from access to Huawei's IPs, which consists of more than 100,000 active patents by the end of 2020, and additionally cannot tap into Huawei's large R&amp;D resources where $20 billion had been committed for 2021. However, <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(magazine)" title="Wired (magazine)">Wired</a></i> magazine noted in 2021 that Honor devices still had not differentiated their software much from Huawei phones and that core apps and certain engineering features, like the Honor-engineered camera features looked "virtually identical' across both phones.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawler_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawler-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:14_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Huawei_phones">History of Huawei phones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: History of Huawei phones"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Huawei_P30.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Huawei_P30.jpg/220px-Huawei_P30.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Huawei_P30.jpg/330px-Huawei_P30.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Huawei_P30.jpg/440px-Huawei_P30.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="2400" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Huawei_P30" title="Huawei P30">Huawei P30</a> with rear triple-lens <a href="/wiki/Leica_Camera" title="Leica Camera">Leica</a> optics camera</figcaption></figure>
<p>In July 2003, Huawei established their handset department and by 2004, Huawei shipped their first phone, the C300. The U626 was Huawei's first 3G phone in June 2005 and in 2006, Huawei launched the first Vodafone-branded 3G handset, the V710. The U8220 was Huawei's first <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> smartphone and was unveiled in <a href="/wiki/Mobile_World_Congress" title="Mobile World Congress">MWC</a> 2009. At <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Electronics_Show" title="Consumer Electronics Show">CES</a> 2012, Huawei introduced the Ascend range starting with the Ascend P1 S. At MWC 2012, Huawei launched the Ascend D1. In September 2012, Huawei launched their first 4G ready phone, the Ascend P1 LTE. At CES 2013, Huawei launched the Ascend D2 and the Ascend Mate. At MWC 2013, the Ascend P2 was launched as the world's first LTE Cat4 smartphone. In June 2013, Huawei launched the Ascend P6 and in December 2013, Huawei introduced Honor as a subsidiary independent brand in China. At CES 2014, Huawei launched the Ascend Mate2 4G in 2014 and at MWC 2014, Huawei launched the MediaPad X1 tablet and Ascend G6 4G smartphone. Other launched in 2014 included the Ascend P7 in May 2014, the Ascend Mate7, the Ascend G7 and the Ascend P7 Sapphire Edition as China's first 4G smartphone with a sapphire screen.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2015, Huawei discontinued the "Ascend" brand for its flagship phones, and launched the new P series with the <a href="/wiki/Huawei_P8" title="Huawei P8">Huawei P8</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Huawei also partnered with Google to build the <a href="/wiki/Nexus_6P" title="Nexus 6P">Nexus 6P</a> which was released in September 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2018, Huawei stated that they will no longer allow unlocking the bootloader of their phones to allow installing third party system software or security updates after Huawei stops them.
<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei is currently the most well-known international corporation in China and a pioneer of the 5G mobile phone standard, which has come to be used globally in the last few years.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laptops">Laptops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Laptops"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Huawei_MateBook_series" title="Huawei MateBook series">Huawei MateBook series</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Huawei_Matebook_2-in-1_tablet_with_Windows_10_(26627094621).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Huawei_Matebook_2-in-1_tablet_with_Windows_10_%2826627094621%29.jpg/220px-Huawei_Matebook_2-in-1_tablet_with_Windows_10_%2826627094621%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Huawei_Matebook_2-in-1_tablet_with_Windows_10_%2826627094621%29.jpg/330px-Huawei_Matebook_2-in-1_tablet_with_Windows_10_%2826627094621%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Huawei_Matebook_2-in-1_tablet_with_Windows_10_%2826627094621%29.jpg/440px-Huawei_Matebook_2-in-1_tablet_with_Windows_10_%2826627094621%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="2912" /></a><figcaption>Huawei Matebook 2-in-1 tablet</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 2016, Huawei entered the laptop markets with the release of its <a href="/wiki/Huawei_MateBook_series" title="Huawei MateBook series">Huawei MateBook series</a> of laptops.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> They have continued to release laptop models in this series into 2020 with their most recent models being the <a href="/wiki/Huawei_MateBook_X_Pro" title="Huawei MateBook X Pro">MateBook X Pro</a> and Matebook 13 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-Bonshor_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bonshor-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tablets">Tablets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Tablets"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Huawei_Mate_series#Tablets" title="Huawei Mate series">Huawei Mate series §&#160;Tablets</a></div>
<p>The Huawei MatePad Pro, launched in November 2019, after that, subsequent releases of their MatePad tablet line.<sup id="cite_ref-HUAWEI_Consumer_2020_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HUAWEI_Consumer_2020-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Huawei is number one in the Chinese tablet market and number two globally as of 4Q 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-Gizchina.com_2020_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gizchina.com_2020-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="PCs">PCs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: PCs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Huawei_MateStation_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Huawei MateStation series">Huawei MateStation series</a></div>
<p>The MateStation S and X was released in September 2021 among successor releases of variants, marking Huawei entrance into the workstation, desktop PC space with All-in-one and Thin client PCs.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wearables">Wearables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Wearables"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Huawei_Watch" title="Huawei Watch">Huawei Watch</a></div>
<p>The <b>Huawei Watch</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Android_Wear" class="mw-redirect" title="Android Wear">Android Wear</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Smartwatch" title="Smartwatch">smartwatch</a> developed by Huawei. It was released at <a href="/wiki/Internationale_Funkausstellung_Berlin" class="mw-redirect" title="Internationale Funkausstellung Berlin">Internationale Funkausstellung Berlin</a> on 2 September 2015. Since 2020, Huawei released subsequent models using in-house operating systems from <a href="/wiki/LiteOS" title="LiteOS">LiteOS</a> powered models to the latest <a href="/wiki/HarmonyOS" title="HarmonyOS">HarmonyOS</a> powered watches.<sup id="cite_ref-twice_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twice-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> It is the first smartwatch produced by Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-twice_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twice-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Their latest watch, Huawei Watch Ultimate Design announced on September 25, 2023, and released 4, October 2023 worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Software">Software</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Software"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="EMUI_.28Emotion_User_Interface.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="EMUI_(Emotion_User_Interface)">EMUI (Emotion User Interface)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: EMUI (Emotion User Interface)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/EMUI" title="EMUI">EMUI</a></div>
<p>Emotion UI (EMUI) was a ROM/OS developed by Huawei Technologies Co. Ltd. and based on Google's Android Open Source Project (AOSP). EMUI is pre-installed on most Huawei Smartphone devices and its subsidiaries the Honor series.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Harmony_OS">Harmony OS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Harmony OS"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/HarmonyOS" title="HarmonyOS">HarmonyOS</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HarmonyOS&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:HMOS_Logo_Icon.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/HMOS_Logo_Icon.svg/220px-HMOS_Logo_Icon.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/HMOS_Logo_Icon.svg/330px-HMOS_Logo_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/HMOS_Logo_Icon.svg/440px-HMOS_Logo_Icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="458" data-file-height="458" /></a><figcaption></figcaption></figure>
<a href="/wiki/HarmonyOS" title="HarmonyOS">HarmonyOS</a> (HMOS) (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans">鸿蒙</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Hóngméng</span></i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Distributed_operating_system" title="Distributed operating system">distributed operating system</a> developed by Huawei for <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_TV" title="Smart TV">smart TVs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_watch" class="mw-redirect" title="Smart watch">smart watches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Personal_computers" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal computers">personal computers</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Smart_device" title="Smart device">smart devices</a>. It has a multi-kernel design with dual frameworks: the operating system selects suitable kernels from the <a href="/wiki/Abstraction_layer" title="Abstraction layer">abstraction layer</a> in the case of devices that use diverse resources.<sup id="cite_ref-HarmonyOS_:0_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarmonyOS_:0-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The operating system was officially launched by Huawei in August 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup></div></div>
<h4><span id="Huawei_Mobile_Services_.28HMS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Huawei_Mobile_Services_(HMS)">Huawei Mobile Services (HMS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Huawei Mobile Services (HMS)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Huawei Mobile Services (HMS) is Huawei's solution to GMS (Google Mobile services) for Android - providing many of the same features for app developers. It also serves as the umbrella brand for Huawei's core set of mobile applications, including <a href="/wiki/Huawei_AppGallery" title="Huawei AppGallery">Huawei AppGallery</a>, which was created as a competitor to <a href="/wiki/Google_Play" title="Google Play">Google's Play Store</a>. In December 2019, Huawei unveiled HMS version 4.0, and as of 16 January 2020, the company reported that it had signed up 55,000 apps using its HMS Core software.<sup id="cite_ref-TechRadar_2020_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TechRadar_2020-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Automobile">Automobile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Automobile"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huawei has secured collaboration with a few automakers including <a href="/wiki/Seres_(automobiles)" title="Seres (automobiles)">Seres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chery" title="Chery">Chery</a>, <a href="/wiki/BAIC_Motor" title="BAIC Motor">BAIC Motor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changan_Automobile" title="Changan Automobile">Changan Automobile</a> and <a href="/wiki/GAC_Group" title="GAC Group">GAC Group</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="AITO">AITO</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: AITO"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/AITO_(marque)" title="AITO (marque)">AITO (marque)</a></div>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/AITO_(marque)" title="AITO (marque)">Aito</a></b> brand (问界 <i>Wenjie</i>) is Huawei's premium EV brand in cooperation with Seres. In December 2021, the <a href="/wiki/AITO_M5" title="AITO M5">AITO M5</a> was unveiled as the first vehicle to be developed in cooperation with Huawei. The model was developed mainly by <a href="/wiki/Seres_(automobiles)" title="Seres (automobiles)">Seres</a> and is essentially a restyled <a href="/wiki/Seres_SF5" title="Seres SF5">Seres SF5</a> crossover.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The model was sold under a new brand called AITO, which stands for "Adding Intelligence to Auto" and uses Huawei DriveONE and HarmonyOS, while the Seres SF5 used Huawei DriveONE and HiCar.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="AVATR">AVATR</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: AVATR"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Avatr_Technology" title="Avatr Technology">Avatr Technology</a></div>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Avatr_Technology" title="Avatr Technology">Avatr</a></b> (阿维塔 <i>Aweita)</i> brand is Huawei's premium EV brand in cooperation with <a href="/wiki/Changan_Automobile" title="Changan Automobile">Changan Automobile</a> and <a href="/wiki/CATL" title="CATL">CATL</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Luxeed">Luxeed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Luxeed"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Luxeed" title="Luxeed">Luxeed</a></div>
<p>The <b>Luxeed</b> (智界 <i>Zhijie</i>) brand is Huawei's premium EV brand in cooperation with Chery, with the first vehicle being the Luxeed S7, previously called the Chery EH3,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> an upcoming premium electric executive sedan due to be unveiled in Q3 2023, and would be the first car to have the Harmony OS 4 system on board.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Huawei_Solar">Huawei Solar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Huawei Solar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huawei entered the <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> (PV) market in 2011, and opened an Energy Center of Competence in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-PV_Mag_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PV_Mag-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> In September 2016, Huawei integrated new manufacturing capabilities into its <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a> hub in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, where it can produce 7,000 inverter units per month.<sup id="cite_ref-PV_Mag_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PV_Mag-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> In October that same year, Huawei entered the <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North American</a> market and formed a strategic partnership with Strata Solar.<sup id="cite_ref-PV_Mag_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PV_Mag-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> In April 2017, Huawei enters the <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_solar_power" title="Rooftop solar power">residential solar</a> market with the launch of its string <a href="/wiki/Solar_inverter" title="Solar inverter">solar inverters</a> and DC <a href="/wiki/Power_optimizer" title="Power optimizer">power optimizers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PV_Mag_145-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PV_Mag-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2022, Huawei is the largest producer of solar inverters in the world with a 29% market share, which saw a significant shipment increase of 83% compared to 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-WoodMac_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WoodMac-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competitive_position">Competitive position</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Competitive position"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Huawei's global growth has largely been driven by its offering of competitive telecommunications equipment at a lesser price than rival firms.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 95">&#58;&#8202;95&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>Huawei Technologies Co. Ltd. was the world's largest telecom equipment maker in 2012<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and China's largest telephone-network equipment maker.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> With 3,442 patents, Huawei became the world's No. 1 applicant for international patents in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In 2019, Huawei had the second most patents granted by the <a href="/wiki/European_Patent_Office" title="European Patent Office">European Patent Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In 2021, the <a href="/wiki/World_Intellectual_Property_Organization" title="World Intellectual Property Organization">World Intellectual Property Organization (WIPO)</a>'s annual <a href="/wiki/World_Intellectual_Property_Indicators" title="World Intellectual Property Indicators">World Intellectual Property Indicators</a> report ranked Huawei's number of patent applications published under the <a href="/wiki/Patent_Cooperation_Treaty" title="Patent Cooperation Treaty">PCT System</a> as 1st in the world, with 5464 patent applications being published during 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-:32_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:32-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2023<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Huawei is the leading 5G equipment manufacturer and has the greatest market share of 5G equipment and has built approximately 70% of worldwide 5G base stations.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 182">&#58;&#8202;182&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_and_development">Research and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Research and development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485"><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" decoding="async" width="42" height="34" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2024</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>As of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, more than half of Huawei's employees are involved in research.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 119">&#58;&#8202;119&#8202;</span></sup> In the same year, Huawei spent $22.1 billion on R&amp;D, around 22.4% of its net sales, being one of the six companies in the world to spend more than $20 billion on R&amp;D spending.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The company has twenty one R&amp;D institutes in countries including China, the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-auto_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Canada,<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Pakistan, Finland, France, Belgium, Germany, Colombia, Sweden, Ireland, India,<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Russia, and Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-auto4_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto5_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei also funds research partnerships with universities such as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_British_Columbia" title="University of British Columbia">University of British Columbia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Waterloo" title="University of Waterloo">University of Waterloo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Western_Ontario" title="University of Western Ontario">University of Western Ontario</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Guelph" title="University of Guelph">University of Guelph</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Universit%C3%A9_Laval" title="Université Laval">Université Laval</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Controversies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Huawei" title="Criticism of Huawei">Criticism of Huawei</a></div>
<p>Huawei has faced allegations that its products contain <a href="/wiki/Backdoor_(computing)" title="Backdoor (computing)">backdoors</a> for Chinese government espionage and domestic laws require Chinese citizens and companies to cooperate with state intelligence when warranted. Huawei executives denied these claims, saying that the company has not received requests by the Chinese government to introduce backdoors in its equipment, would refuse to do so, and that Chinese law does not compel them to do so. As of 2019, the United States had not produced evidence of coordinated hacking by Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-auto8_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto8-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_business_practices">Early business practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Early business practices"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huawei employed a complex system of agreements with local state-owned telephone companies that seemed to include illicit payments to the local telecommunications bureau employees. During the late 1990s, the company created several joint ventures with their state-owned telecommunications company customers. By 1998, Huawei had signed agreements with municipal and provincial telephone bureaus to create Shanghai Huawei, Chengdu Huawei, Shenyang Huawei, Anhui Huawei, Sichuan Huawei, and other companies. The joint ventures were actually shell companies, and were a way to funnel money to local telecommunications employees so that Huawei could get deals to sell them equipment. In the case of Sichuan Huawei, for example, local partners could get 60–70 percent of their investment returned in the form of annual 'dividends'.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allegations_of_state_support">Allegations of state support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Allegations of state support"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Martin Thorley of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">University of Nottingham</a> noted that a "company of Huawei’s size, working in what is considered a sensitive sector, simply cannot succeed in China without extensive links to the Party".<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Klon Kitchen has suggested that 5G dominance is essential to China in order to achieve its vision where "the prosperity of state-run capitalism is combined with the stability and security of technologically enabled authoritarianism".<sup id="cite_ref-Deep_Dive:_The_Geopolitics_of_5G_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deep_Dive:_The_Geopolitics_of_5G-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Nigel Inkster of the International Institute for Strategic Studies suggested that "Huawei involvement in the core backbone 5G infrastructure of developed western liberal democracies is a strategic game-changer because 5G is a game-changer”, with “national telecoms champions” playing a key role, which in turn is part of China's "ambitious strategy to reshape the planet in line with its interests” through the <a href="/wiki/Belt_and_Road_Initiative" title="Belt and Road Initiative">Belt and Road Initiative</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> On 7 October 2020, the U.K. Parliament's <a href="/wiki/Defence_Select_Committee" title="Defence Select Committee">Defence Committee</a> released a report concluding that there was evidence of collusion between Huawei and Chinese state and the Chinese Communist Party, based upon ownership model and government subsidies it has received.<sup id="cite_ref-Corera_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corera-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei has a strong rapport with, and support from, the Chinese government.<sup id="cite_ref-:Tsang&amp;Cheung_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Tsang&amp;Cheung-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 131">&#58;&#8202;131&#8202;</span></sup> The Chinese government has granted Huawei much more comprehensive support than other domestic companies facing troubles abroad, such as <a href="/wiki/ByteDance" title="ByteDance">ByteDance</a>, since Huawei is considered a national champion along with <a href="/wiki/Alibaba_Group" title="Alibaba Group">Alibaba Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tencent" title="Tencent">Tencent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto16_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-asianstudies.org_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asianstudies.org-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> For instance after Huawei CFO Meng Wanzhou was detained in Canada pending extradition to the United States for fraud charges, China immediately arrested Michael Kovrig and Michael Spavor in what was widely viewed as "hostage diplomacy".<sup id="cite_ref-auto16_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> China has also imposed tariffs on Australian imports in 2020, in apparent retaliation for Huawei and ZTE being excluded from Australia's 5G network in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-auto16_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In June 2020, when the UK mulled reversing an earlier decision to permit Huawei's participation in 5G, China threatened retaliation in other sectors by withholding investments in power generation and high-speed rail. A House of Commons defence committee found that "Beijing had exerted pressure through "covert and overt threats" to keep Huawei in the UK's 5G network".<sup id="cite_ref-Corera_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corera-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pompeo" title="Mike Pompeo">Mike Pompeo</a> reassured the UK saying "the US stands with our allies and partners against the Chinese Communist Party's coercive bullying tactics," and "the US stands ready to assist our friends in the UK with any needs they have, from building secure and reliable nuclear power plants to developing trusted 5G solutions that protect their citizens' privacy".<sup id="cite_ref-:26_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:26-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The "optics of Beijing's diplomats coming to [Huawei]'s defense" in the European Union has also contradicted Huawei's claims that it is "fully independent from the Chinese government".<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In November 2019, the Chinese ambassador to Denmark, in meetings with high-ranking <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroese</a> politicians, directly linked Huawei's 5G expansion with Chinese trade, according to a sound recording obtained by <a href="/wiki/Kringvarp_F%C3%B8roya" title="Kringvarp Føroya">Kringvarp Føroya</a>. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Berlingske" title="Berlingske">Berlingske</a></i>, the ambassador threatened with dropping a planned trade deal with the Faroe Islands, if the Faroese telecom company Føroya Tele did not let Huawei build the national 5G network. Huawei said they did not knоw about the meetings.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> China's ambassador to Germany, Wu Ken, warned that ‘there will be consequences’ if Huawei was excluded, and floated the "possibility of German cars being banned on safety grounds".<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>The</i> <i>Wall Street Journal</i> has suggested that Huawei received approximately "$46 billion in loans and other support, coupled with $25 billion in tax cuts" since the Chinese government had a vested interest in fostering a company to compete against Apple and Samsung.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In particular, China's state-owned banks such as the China Development Bank and the <a href="/wiki/Export-Import_Bank_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Export-Import Bank of China">Export-Import Bank of China</a> make loans to Huawei customers which substantially undercut competitors' financing with lower interest and cash in advance, with China Development Bank providing a credit line totaling US$30 billion between 2004 and 2009. In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> launched an investigation into China's subsidies that distorted global markets and harmed European vendors, and Huawei offered the initial complainant US$56 million to withdraw the complaint in an attempt to shut down the investigation. Then-European Trade Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Karel_De_Gucht" title="Karel De Gucht">Karel De Gucht</a> found that Huawei leveraged state support to underbid competitors by up to 70 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allegations_of_military_and_intelligence_ties">Allegations of military and intelligence ties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Allegations of military and intelligence ties"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Concerns_over_Chinese_involvement_in_5G_wireless_networks" title="Concerns over Chinese involvement in 5G wireless networks">Concerns over Chinese involvement in 5G wireless networks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_intelligence_activity_abroad" title="Chinese intelligence activity abroad">Chinese intelligence activity abroad</a></div>
<p>In 2011, a report by the <a href="/wiki/Open_Source_Enterprise" title="Open Source Enterprise">Open Source Enterprise</a> detailed its "suspicions over potential close links between Huawei and the Chinese Government," such as former chairwoman <a href="/wiki/Sun_Yafang" title="Sun Yafang">Sun Yafang</a>'s prior employment by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_State_Security_(China)" title="Ministry of State Security (China)">Ministry of State Security</a> (MSS)'s Communications Department.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:25_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:25-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, Ren Zhengfei stated "we never participate in espionage and we do not allow any of our employees to do any act like that. And we absolutely never install backdoors. Even if we were required by Chinese law, we would firmly reject that".<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Chinese Premier <a href="/wiki/Li_Keqiang" title="Li Keqiang">Li Keqiang</a> was quoted saying "the Chinese government did not and will not ask Chinese companies to spy on other countries, such kind of action is not consistent with the Chinese law and is not how China behaves." Huawei has cited the opinion of Zhong Lun Law Firm, co-signed by a CCP member,<sup id="cite_ref-auto7_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> whose lawyers testified to the <a href="/wiki/FCC" class="mw-redirect" title="FCC">FCC</a> that the <a href="/wiki/National_Intelligence_Law_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="National Intelligence Law of the People&#39;s Republic of China">National Intelligence Law</a> doesn't apply to Huawei. The opinion of Zhong Lun lawyers, reviewed by British law firm Clifford Chance, has been distributed widely by Huawei as an "independent legal opinion", although Clifford Chance added a disclaimer stated that "the material should not be construed as constituting a legal opinion on the application of PRC law".<sup id="cite_ref-auto7_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto12_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto12-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Follow up reporting from <i>Wired</i> cast doubt on the findings of Zhong Lun, particularly because the Chinese "government doesn't limit itself to what the law explicitly allows" when it comes to national security.<sup id="cite_ref-Wired_Simonete_2019_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wired_Simonete_2019-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> "All Chinese citizens and organisations are obliged to cooperate upon request with PRC intelligence operations—and also maintain the secrecy of such operations", as explicitly stipulated in Article 7 of the 2017 PRC national intelligence-gathering activities law.<sup id="cite_ref-auto7_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Tim Rühlig, a Research Fellow at the Swedish Institute of International Affairs, observed that "Not least in the light of the lack of the rule of law in China, but also given the clarity of the Intelligence Law, this legal opinion [by Clifford Chance] does not provide any substantial reassurance that Huawei could decline to cooperate with Chinese intelligence, even if the company wanted to do so".<sup id="cite_ref-ui.se_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ui.se-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Experts have pointed out that under <a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a>'s "intensifying authoritarianism [since] Beijing promulgated a new national intelligence law" in 2017, as well as the 2014 Counter-Espionage Law, both of which are vaguely defined and far-reaching. The two laws "[compel] Chinese businesses to work with Chinese intelligence and security agencies whenever they are requested to do so", suggesting that Huawei or other domestic major technology companies could not refuse to cooperate with Chinese intelligence. Jerome Cohen, a <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a> law professor and Council on Foreign Relations adjunct senior fellow stated "Not only is this mandated by existing legislation but, more important, also by political reality and the organizational structure and operation of the Party-State’s economy. The Party is embedded in Huawei and controls it".<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> One former Huawei employee said "The state wants to use Huawei, and it can use it if it wants. Everyone has to listen to the state. Every person. Every company and every individual, and you can't talk about it. You can't say you don't like it. That's just China."
