The new cybersecurity law also requires domestic companies, and eventually foreign subsidiaries, to use state-certified network equipment and software so that their data and communications are fully visible to China's Cybersecurity Bureau.<sup id="cite_ref-auto16_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto14_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto14-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto7_183-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto12_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto12-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> University of Nottingham's Martin Thorley has suggested that Huawei would have no recourse to oppose the CCP's request in court, since the party controls the police, the media, the judiciary and the government.<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Klon Kitchen has suggested that 5G dominance is essential to China in order to achieve its vision where "the prosperity of state-run capitalism is combined with the stability and security of technologically enabled authoritarianism".<sup id="cite_ref-Deep_Dive:_The_Geopolitics_of_5G_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deep_Dive:_The_Geopolitics_of_5G-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jackson_Society" title="Henry Jackson Society">Henry Jackson Society</a> researchers conducted an analysis of 25,000 Huawei employee CVs and found that some had worked or trained with China's Ministry of State Security, the People's Liberation Army (PLA), its academies, and a military unit accused of hacking US corporations, including 11 alumni from a PLA information engineering school.<sup id="cite_ref-auto10_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto10-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> One of the study researchers says this shows "a strong relationship between Huawei and all levels of the Chinese state, Chinese military and Chinese intelligence. This to me appears to be a systemized, structural relationship."<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In a report by academics Christopher Balding of Fulbright University and Donald C. Clarke of George Washington University, a person "simultaneously held a position at Huawei and a teaching and research role at a military university through which they were employed by the Chinese People’s Liberation Army...a section in the PLA that is responsible for the Chinese military’s space, cyber, and electronic warfare capabilities".<sup id="cite_ref-:18_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Charles Parton, a British diplomat, said this "give the lie to Huawei's claim that there is no evidence that they help the Chinese intelligence services. This gun is smoking."<sup id="cite_ref-auto10_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto10-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Huawei said that while it does not work on Chinese military or intelligence projects, it is no secret that some employees have a previous government background. It criticized the report's speculative language such as ‘believes’, ‘infers’, and ‘cannot rule out’.<sup id="cite_ref-:18_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Global_surveillance_disclosures_(2013%E2%80%93present)#Huawei" class="mw-redirect" title="Global surveillance disclosures (2013–present)">National Security Agency</a> penetrated Huawei's corporate networks in China to search for links between the company and the People's Liberation Army. It was able to monitor accounts belonging to Huawei employees and its founder Ren Zhengfei.<sup id="cite_ref-bnnbloomberg.ca_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bnnbloomberg.ca-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/U.S.-China_Economic_and_Security_Review_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S.-China Economic and Security Review Commission">U.S.-China Economic and Security Review Commission</a> member Michael Wessel said: “If there’s a locksmith who’s installing more and more locks on the doors in a community and suddenly there’s a rash of silent robberies, at some point the locksmith becomes a person of interest. Huawei around that time became a significant entity of interest".<sup id="cite_ref-bnnbloomberg.ca_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bnnbloomberg.ca-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_News" title="Bloomberg News">Bloomberg News</a> report stated that Australian intelligence in 2012 detected a backdoor in the country's telecom network and shared its findings with the United States, who reported similar hacks. It was reportedly caused by a software update from Huawei carrying malicious code that transmitted data to China before deleting itself. Investigators managed to reconstruct the exploit and determined that Huawei technicians must have pushed the update through the network on behalf of China's spy agencies. Huawei said updates would have required authorization from the customer and that no tangible evidence was presented. China's Ministry of Foreign Affairs called the accusation a "slander". Australian telecom operators Optus and Vodafone disputed that they were compromised.<sup id="cite_ref-bnnbloomberg.ca_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bnnbloomberg.ca-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-news.com.au_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.com.au-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Senior security officials in Uganda and Zambia told <i>The Wall Street Journal</i> that Huawei played key roles enabling their governments to spy on political opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-auto16_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Several IT sources told <a href="/wiki/Le_Monde" title="Le Monde"><i>Le Monde</i></a> that inside the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> headquarters, whose computer systems were supplied by Huawei,<sup id="cite_ref-auto16_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> data transfers on its servers peaked after hours from January 2012 to January 2017, with the AU's internal data sent to unknown servers hosted in Shanghai.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> In May 2019, a Huawei Mediapad M5 belonging to a <a href="/wiki/Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian">Canadian</a> IT engineer living in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> was found to be sending data to servers in China despite never being authorized to do so, as the apps could not be disabled and continued to send sensitive data even after appearing to be deleted.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> At the end of 2019, United States officials disclosed to the United Kingdom and Germany that Huawei has had the ability to covertly exploit backdoors intended for law enforcement officials since 2009, as these backdoors are found on carrier equipment like antennas and routers, and Huawei's equipment is widely used around the world due to its low cost.<sup id="cite_ref-Reichert_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reichert-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pancevski_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pancevski-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> The United Kingdom established a lab that it ran, but which was paid for by Huawei, to evaluate Huawei equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-:322_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:322-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 322">&#58;&#8202;322&#8202;</span></sup> After eight years of study, the lab did not identify any Huawei backdoor, but concluded that Huawei's equipment had bugs that could be exploited by hackers.<sup id="cite_ref-:322_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:322-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 322">&#58;&#8202;322&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Timeline"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Yale University economist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_S._Roach" title="Stephen S. Roach">Stephen Roach</a> stated in 2022 that there was no hard evidence to support the allegations of Huawei having a backdoor for industrial espionage other than one arguable instance,<sup id="cite_ref-:15_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 118">&#58;&#8202;118&#8202;</span></sup> which was when UK telecom Vodafone disclosed in 2011 that its Italian fixed line network contained a security vulnerability in its Huawei-installed software.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 118–119">&#58;&#8202;118–119&#8202;</span></sup> Huawei fixed the vulnerability at Vodafone's request.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_153-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 118">&#58;&#8202;118&#8202;</span></sup> There was no report of any suspicious data capture or systems control activity.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:15_153-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Vodafone was satisfied with the outcome and thereafter increased its reliance on Huawei as an equipment-supplier.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_153-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 118">&#58;&#8202;118&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>A 2012 White House-ordered security review found no evidence that Huawei spied for China and said instead that security vulnerabilities on its products posed a greater threat to its users. The details of the leaked review came a week after a US House Intelligence Committee report which warned against letting Huawei supply critical telecommunications infrastructure in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei has been at the center of <a href="/wiki/Concerns_over_Chinese_involvement_in_5G_wireless_networks" title="Concerns over Chinese involvement in 5G wireless networks">concerns over Chinese involvement in 5G wireless networks</a>. In 2018, the United States passed a <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_Authorization_Act_for_Fiscal_Year_2019" title="National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2019">defense funding bill</a> that contained a passage barring the federal government from doing business with Huawei, <a href="/wiki/ZTE" title="ZTE">ZTE</a>, and several Chinese vendors of surveillance products, due to security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> The Chinese government has threatened economic retaliation against countries that block Huawei's market access.<sup id="cite_ref-:26_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:26-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly in November 2018, New Zealand blocked Huawei from supplying mobile equipment to national telecommunications company <a href="/wiki/Spark_New_Zealand" title="Spark New Zealand">Spark New Zealand</a>'s 5G network, citing a "significant network security risk" and concerns about China's <a href="/wiki/National_Intelligence_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="National Intelligence Law">National Intelligence Law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei was involved in developing the United Kingdom's 5G network, which initially led to serious policy and diplomatic disagreements between the UK and the United States, which opposed Huawei's involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-:Curtis&amp;Klaus_64-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Curtis&amp;Klaus-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 77">&#58;&#8202;77&#8202;</span></sup> Between December 2018 and January 2019, German and British intelligence agencies initially pushed back against the US' allegations, stating that after examining Huawei's 5G hardware and accompanying source code, they have found no evidence of malevolence and that a ban would therefore be unwarranted.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the head of Britain's <a href="/wiki/National_Cyber_Security_Centre_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Cyber Security Centre (United Kingdom)">National Cyber Security Centre</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Information_security" title="Information security">information security</a> arm of <a href="/wiki/GCHQ" title="GCHQ">GCHQ</a>) stated that the US has not managed to provide the UK with any proof of its allegations against Huawei and also their agency had concluded that any risks involving Huawei in UK's telecom networks are "manageable".<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The Huawei Cyber Security Evaluation Centre (HCSEC), set up in 2010 to assuage security fears as it examined Huawei hardware and software for the UK market, was staffed largely by employees from Huawei but with regular oversight from GCHQ, which led to questions of operating independence from Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> On 1 October 2020, an official report released by National Cyber Security Centre noted that "Huawei has failed to adequately tackle security flaws in equipment used in the UK's telecoms networks despite previous complaints", and flagged one vulnerability of "national significance" related to broadband in 2019. The report concluded that Huawei was not confident of implementing the five-year plan of improving its software engineering processes, so there was "limited assurance that all risks to UK national security" could be mitigated in the long-term.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> On 14 July 2020, the United Kingdom Government announced a ban on the use of company's 5G network equipment, citing security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In October 2020, the British Defence Select Committee announced that it had found evidence of Huawei's collusion with the Chinese state and that it supported accelerated purging of Huawei equipment from Britain's telecom infrastructure by 2025, since they concluded that Huawei had "engaged in a variety of intelligence, security, and intellectual property activities" despite its repeated denials.<sup id="cite_ref-Corera_169-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corera-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> In November 2020, Huawei challenged the UK government's decision, citing an Oxford Economics report that it had contributed £3.3 billion to the UK's GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2019, Huawei filed three defamation claims over comments suggesting ties to the Chinese government made on television by a French researcher, a broadcast journalist and a telecommunications sector expert.<sup id="cite_ref-theprint.in_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theprint.in-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> In June 2020 ANSSI informed French telecommunications companies that they would not be allowed to renew licenses for 5G equipment made from Huawei after 2028.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> On 28 August 2020, French President <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a> assured the Chinese government that it did not ban Huawei products from participating in its fifth-generation mobile roll-out, but favored European providers for security reasons. The head of the France's cybersecurity agency also stated that it has granted time-limited waivers on 5G for wireless operators that use Huawei products, a decision that likely started a "phasing out" of the company's products.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2020, US government officials claimed that Huawei has had the ability to covertly exploit backdoors intended for law enforcement officials in carrier equipment like antennas and routers since 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Reichert_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reichert-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pancevski_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pancevski-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In mid July 2020, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Little_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="Andrew Little (New Zealand politician)">Andrew Little</a>, the Minister in charge of New Zealand's signals intelligence agency the <a href="/wiki/Government_Communications_Security_Bureau" title="Government Communications Security Bureau">Government Communications Security Bureau</a> (GCSB), announced that New Zealand would not join the United Kingdom and United States in banning Huawei from the country's 5G network.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2022, Canada's industry minister <a href="/wiki/Francois-Philippe_Champagne" class="mw-redirect" title="Francois-Philippe Champagne">Francois-Philippe Champagne</a> announced that Canada will ban Huawei from the country's 5G network, in an effort to protect the safety and security of Canadians, as well as to protect Canada's infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> The Canadian federal government cited national security concerns for the move, saying that the suppliers could be forced to company with "extrajudicial directions from foreign governments" in ways that could "conflict with Canadian laws or would be detrimental to Canadian interests". Telcos will be prevented from procuring new 4G or 5G equipment from Huawei and ZTE and must remove all ZTE- and Huawei-branded 5G equipment from their networks by 28 June 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meng_Wanzhou_case">Meng Wanzhou case</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Meng Wanzhou case"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou" title="Extradition case of Meng Wanzhou">Extradition case of Meng Wanzhou</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Meng_Wanzhou_in_Vancouver_(2021).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Meng_Wanzhou_in_Vancouver_%282021%29.jpg/220px-Meng_Wanzhou_in_Vancouver_%282021%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Meng_Wanzhou_in_Vancouver_%282021%29.jpg/330px-Meng_Wanzhou_in_Vancouver_%282021%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Meng_Wanzhou_in_Vancouver_%282021%29.jpg/440px-Meng_Wanzhou_in_Vancouver_%282021%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1142" data-file-height="890" /></a><figcaption>Meng Wanzhou during her house arrest in Vancouver in 2021</figcaption></figure>
<p>On December 1, 2018, <a href="/wiki/Meng_Wanzhou" title="Meng Wanzhou">Meng Wanzhou</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_directors" title="Board of directors">board</a> <a href="/wiki/Deputy_chairman" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy chairman">deputy chairperson</a> and daughter of <a href="/wiki/Ren_Zhengfei" title="Ren Zhengfei">the founder</a> of the Chinese multinational technology corporation Huawei, was detained upon arrival at <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_International_Airport" title="Vancouver International Airport">Vancouver International Airport</a> by <a href="/wiki/Canada_Border_Services_Agency" title="Canada Border Services Agency">Canada Border Services Agency</a> (CBSA) officers for questioning, which lasted three hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_:9_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_:9-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">Royal Canadian Mounted Police</a> subsequently arrested her on a provisional <a href="/wiki/Extradition_law_in_the_United_States" title="Extradition law in the United States">U.S. extradition request</a> for fraud and conspiracy to commit fraud in order to circumvent <a href="/wiki/United_States_sanctions_against_Iran" title="United States sanctions against Iran">U.S. sanctions against Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_:9_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_:9-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> On January 28, 2019, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a> formally announced financial <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a> charges against Meng.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> The first stage of the <a href="/wiki/Extradition" title="Extradition">extradition</a> <a href="/wiki/Hearing_(law)" title="Hearing (law)">hearing</a> for Meng began Monday, January 20, 2020, and concluded on May 27, 2020, when the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_British_Columbia" title="Supreme Court of British Columbia">Supreme Court of British Columbia</a> ordered the extradition to proceed.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the extradition courtroom proceedings, Meng's lawyers made several allegations against the prosecution, including allegations of <a href="/wiki/Unlawful_detention" class="mw-redirect" title="Unlawful detention">unlawful detention</a> of Meng,<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> unlawful <a href="/wiki/Search_and_seizure" title="Search and seizure">search and seizure</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto10_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto10-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> extradition law violations,<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto5_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto5-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Misrepresentation" title="Misrepresentation">misrepresentation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto6_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto6-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto8_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto8-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto1_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto1-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a> violation,<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto3_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto3-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Perjury" title="Perjury">fabricated testimonies</a> by the CBSA,<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto7_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto7-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> each of which were responded to by the prosecution.<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto9_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto9-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto4_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto4-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto2_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto2-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto11_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_auto11-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> In August 2021, the extradition judge questioned the regularity of the case and expressed great difficulty in understanding how the Record of Case (ROC) presented by the US supported their allegation of criminality.<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_Smart_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_Smart-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
On September 24, 2021, the Department of Justice announced it had reached a deal with Meng to resolve the case through a deferred prosecution agreement. As part of the deal, Meng agreed to a statement of facts that said she had made untrue statements to <a href="/wiki/HSBC" title="HSBC">HSBC</a> to enable transactions in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, at least some of which supported Huawei's work in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> in violation of U.S. sanctions, but did not have to pay a fine nor plead guilty to her key charges.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> The Department of Justice said it would move to dismiss all charges against Meng when the deferral period ends on 21 December 2022, on the condition that Meng is not charged with a crime before then.<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_cut_a_deal_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_cut_a_deal-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Meng was released from <a href="/wiki/House_arrest" title="House arrest">house arrest</a> and left Canada for China on September 24, 2021; hours after news of the deal, <a href="/wiki/Detention_of_Michael_Spavor_and_Michael_Kovrig" title="Detention of Michael Spavor and Michael Kovrig">Michael Spavor and Michael Kovrig</a>, two Canadian citizens whose arrest in mainland China were widely seen as retaliation for Meng's arrest in Canada, were released from detention in China and flown back to Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> On December 2, 2022, the presiding judge dismissed the charges against Meng following the <a href="/wiki/United_States_government" class="mw-redirect" title="United States government">United States government</a>'s request.<sup id="cite_ref-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_Chen_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extradition_case_of_Meng_Wanzhou_Chen-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intellectual_property_infringement">Intellectual property infringement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Intellectual property infringement"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Allegations_of_intellectual_property_theft_by_China" title="Allegations of intellectual property theft by China">Allegations of intellectual property theft by China</a></div>
<p>Huawei has settled with <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Cisco Systems">Cisco Systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Motorola" title="Motorola">Motorola</a>, and PanOptis in patent infringement lawsuits.<sup id="cite_ref-:19_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:19-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> In 2018, a German court ruled against Huawei and ZTE in favor of <a href="/wiki/MPEG_LA" title="MPEG LA">MPEG LA</a>, which holds patents related to <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Video_Coding" title="Advanced Video Coding">Advanced Video Coding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huawei has been accused of <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property_theft" class="mw-redirect" title="Intellectual property theft">intellectual property theft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> In February 2003, Cisco Systems sued Huawei Technologies for allegedly infringing on its patents and illegally copying source code used in its routers and switches.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (October 2023)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> By July 2004, Huawei removed the contested code, manuals and command-line interfaces and the case was subsequently settled out of court.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> As part of the settlement Huawei admitted that it had copied some of Cisco's router software.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_2019_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_2019-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the 2004 Supercomm tech conference in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, a Huawei employee allegedly opened up the networking equipment of other companies to photograph the circuit boards.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_2019_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_2019-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nationalpost.com_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalpost.com-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Brian Shields, former chief security officer at Nortel, said that his company was compromised in 2004 by Chinese hackers; executive credentials were accessed remotely, and entire computers were taken over. Shields does not believe Huawei was directly involved but thinks that Huawei was a beneficiary of the hack. Documents taken included product roadmaps, sales proposals, and technical papers.<sup id="cite_ref-nationalpost.com_253-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalpost.com-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> Nortel sought for but failed to receive help from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">Royal Canadian Mounted Police</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Security_Intelligence_Service" title="Canadian Security Intelligence Service">Canadian Security Intelligence Service</a> said it approached the company but was rebuffed.<sup id="cite_ref-Marlow_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marlow-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kehoe_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kehoe-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cybersecurity experts have some doubts about a hack of such magnitude as described by Shields, calling it "unlikely".<sup id="cite_ref-Marlow_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marlow-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> An extensive analysis by University of Ottawa professor Jonathan Calof and recollections of former Nortel executive Tim Dempsey place the blame mostly on strategic mistakes and poor management at Nortel. On the other hand, some employees recall when Huawei or a front company returned a fibre card to Nortel disassembled, around a time when knock-offs products emerged in Asia. There remains a suspicion that industrial espionage brought down or at least accelerated Nortel's demise.<sup id="cite_ref-nationalpost.com_253-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalpost.com-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, a jury found that Huawei had misappropriated trade secrets of <a href="/wiki/T-Mobile_US" title="T-Mobile US">T-Mobile US</a> but awarded damages only for a breach of supplier contract; it did not compensate T-Mobile for claims of espionage.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2020, the United States Department of Justice charged Huawei with <a href="/wiki/Racketeer_Influenced_and_Corrupt_Organizations_Act" title="Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act">racketeering</a> and conspiring to steal trade secrets from six US firms.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Huawei said those allegations, some going back almost 20 years, had never been found as a basis for any significant monetary judgment.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:12_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Korea">North Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: North Korea"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Leaked documents obtained by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> in 2019 raised questions about whether Huawei conducted business secretly with North Korea, which was under numerous US sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Xinjiang_internment_camps">Xinjiang internment camps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Xinjiang internment camps"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Uyghurs_in_China" title="Persecution of Uyghurs in China">Persecution of Uyghurs in China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_internment_camps" title="Xinjiang internment camps">Xinjiang internment camps</a></div>
<p>Huawei has been accused of providing technology used in the <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_China" title="Mass surveillance in China">mass surveillance</a> and detention of <a href="/wiki/Uyghurs" title="Uyghurs">Uyghurs</a> in <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_internment_camps" title="Xinjiang internment camps">Xinjiang internment camps</a>, resulting in sanctions by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">United States Department of State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto13_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto13-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto11_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto11-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Documents show that it has developed facial recognition software that recognizes ethnicity-specific features for surveillance<sup id="cite_ref-auto15_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto15-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> and filed a patent in <a href="/wiki/China_National_Intellectual_Property_Administration" title="China National Intellectual Property Administration">China</a> for a technology that could identify <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han</a> and Uyghur pedestrians.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> The company and its suppliers have also been accused of using forced labor.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> Huawei denied operating such technology.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_use_by_Hamas">Alleged use by Hamas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Alleged use by Hamas"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 8 October 2023, former <a href="/wiki/MI6" title="MI6">MI6</a> spy <a href="/wiki/Aimen_Dean" title="Aimen Dean">Aimen Dean</a> posted on <a href="/wiki/X_(website)" class="mw-redirect" title="X (website)">X</a> that Israel's failure to detect the <a href="/wiki/2023_Hamas-led_attack_on_Israel" title="2023 Hamas-led attack on Israel">Hamas-led attack on Israel</a> was due partly to its militants use of Huawei phones, tablets and laptops, elaborating that US tech companies barring of Huawei had forced it to develop its own systems that were not easy to hack except by China.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawsuit">Lawsuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Lawsuit"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In January 2024, <a href="/wiki/Netgear" title="Netgear">Netgear</a>, a computer networking company based in San Jose, California, filed a lawsuit with a California federal court against Huawei, claiming the company broke the United States <a href="/wiki/Competition_law" title="Competition law">antitrust law</a> by withholding patent licenses, in addition to allegations of <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Racketeering" title="Racketeering">racketeering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="NSA_infiltration">NSA infiltration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: NSA infiltration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2014, <i><a href="/wiki/Der_Spiegel_(website)" title="Der Spiegel (website)">Der Spiegel</a></i> and <i>The New York Times</i> reported that, according to <a href="/wiki/2010s_global_surveillance_disclosures#Huawei" title="2010s global surveillance disclosures">global surveillance disclosures</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> (NSA) infiltrated Huawei's computer network in 2009. The White House intelligence coordinator and the <a href="/wiki/FBI" class="mw-redirect" title="FBI">FBI</a> were also involved. The operation obtained Huawei's customer list and internal training documents. In addition, the company's central email archive was accessed, including messages from founder Ren Zhengfei and chairwoman Sun Yafang. So much data was gathered that "we don't know what to do with it", according to one document. The NSA was concerned that Huawei's infrastructure could provide China with <a href="/wiki/Signals_intelligence" title="Signals intelligence">signals intelligence</a> capabilities. It also wanted to find ways to exploit the company's products because they are used by targets of interest to the NSA.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Sanctions.2C_bans.2C_and_restrictions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sanctions,_bans,_and_restrictions">Sanctions, bans, and restrictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Sanctions, bans, and restrictions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: United States"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_States_sanctions_against_China" title="United States sanctions against China">United States sanctions against China</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_the_2020_semiconductor_ban">Before the 2020 semiconductor ban</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Before the 2020 semiconductor ban"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In August 2018, the <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_Authorization_Act_for_Fiscal_Year_2019" title="National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2019">National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2019</a> (NDAA 2019) was signed into law, containing a provision that banned Huawei and ZTE equipment from being used by the US federal government, citing security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> Huawei filed a lawsuit over the act in March 2019,<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> alleging it to be unconstitutional because it specifically targeted Huawei without granting it a chance to provide a rebuttal or <a href="/wiki/Due_process" title="Due process">due process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, on 15 May 2019, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Commerce" title="United States Department of Commerce">Department of Commerce</a> added Huawei and 70 foreign subsidiaries and "affiliates" to its <a href="/wiki/Entity_List" title="Entity List">Entity List</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Export_Administration_Regulations" title="Export Administration Regulations">Export Administration Regulations</a>, citing the company having been indicted for "knowingly and willfully causing the export, re-export, sale and supply, directly and indirectly, of goods, technology and services (banking and other financial services) from the United States to Iran and the government of Iran without obtaining a license from the Department of Treasury's <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Foreign_Assets_Control" title="Office of Foreign Assets Control">Office of Foreign Assets Control</a> (OFAC)".<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> This restricts US companies from doing business with Huawei without a government license.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Various US-based companies immediately froze their business with Huawei to comply with the regulation.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The May 2019 ban on Huawei was partial: it did not affect most non-American produced chips, and the Trump administration granted a series of extensions on the ban in any case,<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> with another 90-day reprieve issued in May 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> In May 2020, the US extended the ban to cover semiconductors customized for Huawei and made with US technology.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> In August 2020, the US again extended the ban to a blanket ban on all semiconductor sales to Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_281-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> The blanket ban took effect in September 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> Samsung and LG Display were banned from supplying displays to Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="After_2020">After 2020</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: After 2020"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The sanctions regime established in September 2020 negatively affected Huawei production, sales and financial projections.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> However, on 29 June 2019 at the <a href="/wiki/2019_G20_Osaka_summit" title="2019 G20 Osaka summit">G20 summit</a>, the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">US President</a> made statements implicating plans to ease the restrictions on US companies doing business with Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_June_29,_2019_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_June_29,_2019-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> Despite this statement, on 15 May 2020, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Commerce" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Commerce">U.S. Department of Commerce</a> extended its export restrictions to bar Huawei from producing semiconductors derived from technology or software of US origin, even if the manufacturing is performed overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> In June 2020, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC) designated Huawei a national security threat, thereby barring it from any US subsidies.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> In July 2020, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Acquisition_Regulation" title="Federal Acquisition Regulation">Federal Acquisition Regulation</a> Council published a <a href="/wiki/Federal_Register" title="Federal Register">Federal Register</a> notice prohibiting all federal government contractors from selling Huawei hardware to the federal government and preventing federal contractors from using Huawei hardware.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2020, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> issued an <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_13959" title="Executive Order 13959">executive order</a> prohibiting any American company or individual from owning shares in companies that the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a> has listed as having links to the People's Liberation Army, which included Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> In January 2021, the Trump administration revoked licenses from US companies such as <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> from supplying products and technologies to Huawei.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> In June 2021, the FCC voted unanimously to prohibit approvals of Huawei gear in US telecommunication networks on national security grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2021, the <a href="/wiki/Administration_of_Joe_Biden" class="mw-redirect" title="Administration of Joe Biden">administration of Joe Biden</a> began to persuade the United Arab Emirates to remove the Huawei Technologies Co. equipment from its telecommunications network, while ensuring to further distance itself from China. It came as an added threat to the $23 billion arms deal of <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_F-35_Lightning_II" title="Lockheed Martin F-35 Lightning II">F-35 fighter jets</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Atomics_MQ-9_Reaper" title="General Atomics MQ-9 Reaper">Reaper drones</a> between the US and the UAE. The Emirates got a deadline of four years from Washington to replace the Chinese network.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> A report in September 2021 analyzed how the UAE was struggling between maintaining its relations with both the United States and China. While Washington had a hawkish stance towards Beijing, the increasing Emirati relations with China have strained those with America. In that light, the Western nation has raised concerns for the UAE to beware of the security threat that the Chinese technologies like Huawei 5G telecommunications network possessed. However, the Gulf nations like the Emirates and Saudi Arabia defended their decision of picking Chinese technology over the American, saying that it is much cheaper and had no political conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 25 November 2022, the FCC issued a ban on Huawei for national security reasons, citing the national security risk posed by the technology owned by China.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> In May 2024, the U.S. Department of Commerce revoked some export licenses that allow Intel and Qualcomm to supply Huawei with semiconductors.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Huawei.27s_reaction"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Huawei's_reaction">Huawei's reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Huawei&#039;s reaction"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Stockpiling_of_processors">Stockpiling of processors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Stockpiling of processors"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Before the 15 September 2020 deadline, Huawei was in "survival mode" and stockpiled "5G mobile processors, Wifi, radio frequency and display driver chips and other components" from key chip suppliers and manufacturers, including Samsung, <a href="/wiki/SK_Hynix" title="SK Hynix">SK Hynix</a>, <a href="/wiki/TSMC" title="TSMC">TSMC</a>, <a href="/wiki/MediaTek" title="MediaTek">MediaTek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Realtek" title="Realtek">Realtek</a>, Novatek, and RichWave.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_282-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> Even in 2019, Huawei spent $23.45 billion on the stockpiling of chips and other supplies in 2019, up 73% from 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_282-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> In May 2020, SMIC manufactured 14nm chips for Huawei, which was the first time Huawei used a foundry other than TSMC.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> In July 2020, TSMC confirmed it would halt the shipment of silicon wafers to Chinese telecommunications equipment manufacturer Huawei and its subsidiary <a href="/wiki/HiSilicon" title="HiSilicon">HiSilicon</a> by 14 September.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On its most crucial business, namely, its telecoms business (including 5G) and server business, Huawei has stockpiled 1.5 to 2 years' worth of chips and components.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> It began massively stockpiling from 2018, when Meng Wanzhou, the daughter of Huawei's founder, was arrested in Canada upon US request.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_308-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> Key Huawei suppliers included Xilinx, Intel, AMD, Samsung, SK Hynix, Micron and Kioxia.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_308-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, analysts predicted that Huawei could ship 195 million units of smartphones from its existing stockpile in 2021, but shipments may drop to 50 million in 2021 if rules are not relaxed.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_282-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_processors">Development of processors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Development of processors"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In late 2020, it was reported that Huawei had planned to build a semiconductor manufacturing facility in Shanghai that did not involve US technology.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> The plan may have helped Huawei obtain necessary chips after its existing stockpile became depleted, which would have helped the company chart a sustainable path for its telecoms business.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_309-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Huawei had also planned to collaborate with the government-run Shanghai IC R&amp;D Center, which is partially owned by the state-owned enterprise <a href="/wiki/Hua_Hong_Semiconductor" title="Hua Hong Semiconductor">Hua Hong Semiconductor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_309-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Huawei may have been purchasing equipment from Chinese firms such as AMEC and <a href="/wiki/NAURA_Technology_Group" title="NAURA Technology Group">NAURA Technology Group</a>, as well as using foreign tools which it could still find on the market.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_309-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2023, the <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_Industry_Association" title="Semiconductor Industry Association">Semiconductor Industry Association</a> (SIA), a US trade association, alleged that Huawei was building a collection of secret semiconductor-fabrication facilities across China, a shadow manufacturing network that would let the company skirt US sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fox_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fox-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> Huawei was receiving an estimated $30 billion in state funding from the government at the time and had acquired at least two existing plants, with plans to construct at least three others.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_310-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fox_312-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fox-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> The United States Department of Commerce had put Huawei on its entity list in 2019,<sup id="cite_ref-Fox_312-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fox-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> eventually "prohibiting it from working with American companies in almost all circumstances." However, if Huawei were to function under the names of other companies without disclosing its own involvement, it might have been able to circumvent those restrictions to "indirectly purchase American chipmaking equipment and other supplies that would otherwise be prohibited."<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_310-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 September 2023, Huawei launched its new <a href="/wiki/Huawei_Mate_60" title="Huawei Mate 60">Mate 60</a> smartphone.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> The phone is powered by a new Kirin 9000s chip, made in China by <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_Manufacturing_International_Corporation" title="Semiconductor Manufacturing International Corporation">Semiconductor Manufacturing International Corp</a> (SMIC).<sup id="cite_ref-:17_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> This processor was the first to use the new 7&#160;nanometre SMIC technology. TechInsights had stated in 2022 that it believed SMIC had managed to produce 7&#160;nm chips, even though faced by a harsh sanctions regime, by adapting simpler machines that it could still purchase from <a href="/wiki/ASML_Holding" title="ASML Holding">ASML</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:17_314-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> Holger Mueller of Constellation Research Inc. said that this showed that the US sanctions might have had the effect of sending China's chip-making industry into overdrive: "If SMIC really has perfected its 7nm process, this would be a major advance that can help Huawei remain at the forefront of the smartphone industry."<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> TechInsights found evidence that the processor had been manufactured using SMIC's <a href="/wiki/7_nm_process#7_nm_process_nodes_and_process_offerings" title="7 nm process">N+2</a> 7&#160;nm node.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> One of its analysts, Dan Hutcheson, who had led the breakdown of the new device, stated that it demonstrates "impressive technical progress China's semiconductor industry has made" despite not having <a href="/wiki/EUVL" class="mw-redirect" title="EUVL">EUVL</a> tools, and that "the difficulty of this achievement also shows the resilience of the country's chip technological ability". However other analysts have said that such an achievement may lead to harsher sanctions against it.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Replacement_operating_systems">Replacement operating systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Replacement operating systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Deepin" title="Deepin">Deepin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harmony_OS" class="mw-redirect" title="Harmony OS">Harmony OS</a></div>
<p>After the US sanctions regime started in summer 2018, Huawei started working on its own in-house operating system codenamed "<a href="/wiki/HongMeng_OS" class="mw-redirect" title="HongMeng OS">HongMeng OS</a>": in an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Die_Welt" title="Die Welt">Die Welt</a></i>, executive Richard Yu stated in 2019 that an in-house OS could be used as a "plan B" if it were prevented from using Android or Windows as the result of US action.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Huawei filed trademarks for the names "Ark", "Ark OS", and "Harmony" in Europe, which were speculated to be connected to this OS.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> On 9 August 2019, Huawei officially unveiled Harmony OS at its inaugural HDC developers' conference in Dongguan with the ARK compiler which can be used to port Android APK packages to the OS.<sup id="cite_ref-engadget.com_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engadget.com-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-verge-harmony_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verge-harmony-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2019, Huawei began offering the Linux distribution <a href="/wiki/Deepin" title="Deepin">Deepin</a> as a pre-loaded operating system on selected Matebook models in China.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Whereas at first the official Huawei line was that Harmony&#160;OS was not intended for smartphones, in June 2021 Huawei began shipping its smartphones<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> with Harmony&#160;OS by default in China (in Europe it kept Android, in its own version <a href="/wiki/EMUI" title="EMUI">EMUI</a>, as the default). The operating system proved a success in China, rising from no market share at all to 10&#160;per cent of the Chinese market for smartphones within two years (from mid-2021 to mid-2023), at the expense of Android.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_countries">Other countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Other countries"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2013, Taiwan blocked <a href="/wiki/Mobile_network_operator" title="Mobile network operator">mobile network operators</a> and government departments from using Huawei equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2018, Japan banned Huawei from receiving government contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-Japan_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Japan-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:23_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-329">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, Vietnam left Huawei out of bids to build the country's 5G network out of national security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24-330">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the initial <a href="/wiki/2020%E2%80%932021_China%E2%80%93India_skirmishes" title="2020–2021 China–India skirmishes">2020–2021 China–India skirmishes</a>, India announced that Huawei telecommunication gear would be removed from the country and that the company would be blocked from participating in India's 5G network out of national security concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-:21_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:21-332">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-India_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-India-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ten out of the 27 <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> member states have regulatory frameworks curbing Huawei products. They range from bans, higher barriers to approval, refusal to renew licenses, and unimplemented proposals.<sup id="cite_ref-Europeancountries_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europeancountries-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Having previously banned Huawei from participating in its 5G auction, Brazil reversed its position in early 2021 and allowed Huawei to participate.<sup id="cite_ref-:Tsang&amp;Cheung_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:Tsang&amp;Cheung-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 131">&#58;&#8202;131&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>In May 2022, Canada's government banned Huawei and ZTE equipment from the country's 5G network, with network operators having until 28 June 2024 to remove what they had already installed. The ban followed years of lobbying from the US, part of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Eyes" title="Five Eyes">Five Eyes</a> intelligence alliance that also includes Australia, Canada, New Zealand, and the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> Australia and New Zealand have also banned or restricted Huawei products.<sup id="cite_ref-channele2e_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-channele2e-336">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2022, the UK extended the deadline by a year to the end of 2023 for removing core Huawei equipment from network functions. The ban, originally announced in 2020 following US pressure, calls for the phasing out of all Huawei gear from UK's 5G network by the end of 2027, which remains unchanged.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Per an August 2023 decree on 5G network development, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> barred firms from all countries that have not signed the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Cybercrime" title="Convention on Cybercrime">Budapest Convention on cybercrime</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-338">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> The decree affects Chinese firms like Huawei, as well as firms from South Korea, Russia and Brazil, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_338-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-338">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_view">Chinese view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Chinese view"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Western distrust and targeting of Huawei is generally viewed by the Chinese public as unjustified.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 66">&#58;&#8202;66&#8202;</span></sup> This has led to a perspective in the Chinese public and among city governments that patronizing Huawei helps support China in geopolitical and technological competition with the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_147-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 66">&#58;&#8202;66&#8202;</span></sup> Huawei has thus received high levels of support in terms of public sentiment which its rival firms do not benefit from to the same extent.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_147-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 66">&#58;&#8202;66&#8202;</span></sup> Huawei's top position in China's <a href="/wiki/Smart_city" title="Smart city">smart cities</a> technology market has in particular been boosted by these sentiments.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_147-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 66">&#58;&#8202;66&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Huawei&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
